Sunni Definition of Ru'yah: A Created Power
Sanusi defines ru'yah as a created power and distinguishes customary conditions from necessary ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of ru'yah as a created power that does not require physical conditions, making the vision of Allah possible without direction.

Core Idea
The vision of Allah is possible because it is a power He creates, not a physical act.

This lesson establishes the foundational Sunni position on seeing Allah in the afterlife. The author defines what vision (ru'yah) really means according to Ahl al-Sunnah and clarifies that the usual prerequisites like distance and direction are merely customary, not essential. Understanding this definition is key to accepting the possibility of ru'yah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni position
Read the opening lines of the Arabic passage where the Sunni stance is stated. Notice that they affirm ru'yah is possible but transcendent.
2
Examine the definition of ru'yah
Focus on the phrase: 'الرؤية على مذهب أهل الحق: قوة يجعلها الله تعالى في خلقه'. Understand that vision is a power created by Allah, not a physical process.
The definition is crucial to refute Mu'tazilite objections.
3
Understand the distinction between customary and necessary conditions
The passage says the usual conditions (rays, facing) are 'على جهة الاتفاق لا على سبيل الاشتراط'. Grasp that these conditions are only conventional, not intellectually required.
This opens the door for seeing Allah without direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنَّ أهلَ السُّنَّة ذهبوا إلى أنَّه تعالى يجوز أن يُرى ، والمؤمنون في الجنَّة يَرونَهُ مُنزَّها عن المُقابلة والجهة والمكان ؛ إذْ الرُّؤية على مذهب أهل الحقِّ: قُوَّة يجعلها الله تعالى في خلقه، لا يُشترط فيها اتصال الأشعة ، ولا مُقابلة المرئي، ولا غير ذلك، ولكن جرت العادة في رؤية بعضنا بعضاً بوجود ذلك، على جهة الاتفاق، لا على سبيل الاشتراط؛ فلذا كانت الرُّؤية جائزة لإمكانها بدليل السَّمع المشار إليه بقوله: (إِذْ بِجَائِز عُلّقتْ) ولا يلزم من رؤيته تعالى إثباتُ جهةٍ تعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرًا! بل يراه المؤمنونَ لا في جهةٍ ، كما يعلمون أنه لا في جهةٍ. وقد كان العلماء من أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يختلفون في وقوع رؤية المؤمنين ربهم في الآخرة ، وكذلك من بعدهم من أهل العلم ؛ قال الإمام مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه: لما حجب أعداءه فلم يروه، تجلّى لأوليائه حتى رأوه، ولو لم ير المؤمنون ربهم يوم القيامة لم يعير الكافرون بالحجاب، قال تعالى: (كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ) [المطففين: 15] وقال الإمام الشافعي: لما حجب قوما بالسخط دل على أن قوما يرونه بالرضا. وخالف في ذلك جميع الفرق، فأحالها المعتزلة، بناء على أنها لا تتعلق عقلا إلا بما هو في جهة، ومكان، ومسافة مخصوصة، متمسكين
Indeed, the People of the Sunnah hold that He, exalted, may be seen, and the believers in Paradise see Him, transcendent above facing, direction, and place; for the vision according to the people of truth is a power that Allah creates in His creation, in which the connection of rays is not required, nor facing the seen object, nor anything else, but the custom in seeing some of us others occurs with that, by way of agreement, not by way of necessity; therefore the vision is permissible due to its possibility by the evidence of revelation indicated by his saying: 'For it is attached to the possible.' And it does not follow from seeing Him, exalted, the affirmation of direction – exalted is Allah above that greatly! Rather, the believers see Him not in a direction, just as they know that He is not in a direction. And the scholars among the Companions of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, did not differ concerning the occurrence of the believers' seeing their Lord in the Hereafter, and likewise those after them among the people of knowledge. Imam Malik, may Allah be pleased with him, said: When He veiled His enemies so they did not see Him, He manifested to His friends until they saw Him. And if the believers did not see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection, the disbelievers would not be reproached for the veil; Allah said: 'Nay, indeed they from their Lord on that day are veiled' (Surah Al-Mutaffifin: 15). And Imam Al-Shafi'i said: When He veiled a people with wrath, it indicates that a people see Him with pleasure. And all the sects opposed this; the Mu'tazila deemed it impossible, based on the fact that vision mentally pertains only to what is in a direction, a place, and a specific distance, clinging to [that].
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition, proof, and refutation. It begins by stating the Sunni position: Allah may be seen in the afterlife, but in a manner transcendent of physical direction or location. The author then defines the act of seeing (ru'yah) as a power (quwwa) that Allah creates in His creatures, not requiring the physical conditions like light rays or facing the object; those conditions are merely customary ('ādī) not intellectually necessary (ʻaqlī). This definition makes the vision of Allah possible without any physical attributes. The author then provides evidence from revelation (samʿ): the possibility of vision is supported by scriptural indications. He adds that affirming vision does not imply direction (jiha). Next, he cites the consensus of early scholars, including Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafiʿi, who argued that if believers did not see Allah, the veiling of disbelievers would not be a reproach. Finally, he states that all sects (firaq) opposed the Sunni view; the Mu'tazila denied vision based on the assumption that seeing something requires it to be in a direction and place – a condition they considered rationally necessary, but the Sunnis hold is only customary.

Key Terms
الرؤية
القوة
الاتفاق
الاشتراط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming vision of Allah means He is in a physical direction or place.
Correction: The passage defines vision as a power not requiring direction, so affirmation of ru'yah does not imply direction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of the conditions for seeing in this world (like rays and facing)?
A) They are necessary (ishtirāṭ).
B) They are customary (ittifāq).
C) They are both necessary and customary.
D) They are irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni definition of ru'yah as a created power frees vision from physical constraints, making it possible to see Allah without direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife, do you unconsciously imagine a spatial 'meeting'? How does this lesson's definition of ru'yah as a created power without direction challenge that mental picture, and what must you now unlearn?
?
If the vision of Allah depends entirely on His creating a power in you, not on your own physical or spiritual effort, how does that affect your understanding of your ultimate closeness to Him? Is your hope of seeing Him rooted in His mercy or in your own imagined worthiness?
?
The passage says customary conditions in worldly vision are 'by agreement, not by necessity.' Are there habits in your daily faith practice that you treat as necessary for your relationship with Allah, but are merely customary? What would it cost you to release that assumption?

Scriptural and Scholarly Evidence for Ru'yah
Sanusi presents consensus and scriptural evidence for ru'yah and outlines the Mu'tazilite objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and scholarly evidence for the Sunni doctrine of ru'yah and the Mu'tazilite objection based on their rational assumption.

Core Idea
Consensus of early scholars and the Quranic verse on veiling affirm that believers will see Allah.

After establishing the definition of ru'yah, the author provides evidence from revelation and scholarly consensus. He quotes Imam Malik and Imam al-Shafi'i, who argued from the Quranic verse that veiling implies vision. He then notes that all other sects opposed, especially the Mu'tazila, who claimed that vision is impossible because it requires direction and place. This lesson examines these proofs and the opposing argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand that ru'yah does not imply direction
Read the statement: 'بل يراه المؤمنون لا في جهة'. Understand that affirming vision does not entail attributing direction to Allah.
This echoes the earlier definition.
2
Examine the consensus of early scholars
The passage states that the Companions and later scholars did not differ on this. Reflect on the weight of ijma' (consensus).
3
Analyze the arguments from Malik and Shafi'i
Read the quoted statements. Malik: the veiling of enemies implies manifestation for friends. Shafi'i: veiling with wrath indicates seeing with pleasure.
These are logical inferences from the Quranic text.
4
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection
The Mu'tazila rejected ru'yah because they held that vision only applies to objects in a direction. Note that the Sunni response is already given in the definition that conditions are customary.
This objection is based on a rational assumption, not revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنَّ أهلَ السُّنَّة ذهبوا إلى أنَّه تعالى يجوز أن يُرى ، والمؤمنون في الجنَّة يَرونَهُ مُنزَّها عن المُقابلة والجهة والمكان ؛ إذْ الرُّؤية على مذهب أهل الحقِّ: قُوَّة يجعلها الله تعالى في خلقه، لا يُشترط فيها اتصال الأشعة ، ولا مُقابلة المرئي، ولا غير ذلك، ولكن جرت العادة في رؤية بعضنا بعضاً بوجود ذلك، على جهة الاتفاق، لا على سبيل الاشتراط؛ فلذا كانت الرُّؤية جائزة لإمكانها بدليل السَّمع المشار إليه بقوله: (إِذْ بِجَائِز عُلّقتْ) ولا يلزم من رؤيته تعالى إثباتُ جهةٍ تعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرًا! بل يراه المؤمنونَ لا في جهةٍ ، كما يعلمون أنه لا في جهةٍ. وقد كان العلماء من أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يختلفون في وقوع رؤية المؤمنين ربهم في الآخرة ، وكذلك من بعدهم من أهل العلم ؛ قال الإمام مالك رضي الله تعالى عنه: لما حجب أعداءه فلم يروه، تجلّى لأوليائه حتى رأوه، ولو لم ير المؤمنون ربهم يوم القيامة لم يعير الكافرون بالحجاب، قال تعالى: (كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ) [المطففين: 15] وقال الإمام الشافعي: لما حجب قوما بالسخط دل على أن قوما يرونه بالرضا. وخالف في ذلك جميع الفرق، فأحالها المعتزلة، بناء على أنها لا تتعلق عقلا إلا بما هو في جهة، ومكان، ومسافة مخصوصة، متمسكين

According to the passage, what was the basis for the Mu'tazilite denial of ru'yah?
A) They rejected the Quranic verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The early scholars never differed on ru'yah—they were certain. Do you have that same certainty in your heart? If not, what is holding you back from full conviction? Will you seek to strengthen your faith by studying the evidences more deeply?
?
Imam Malik used the veiling of disbelievers to prove that believers see Allah. When you contemplate standing before your Lord, does your heart yearn for that vision more than any worldly pleasure? If not, what does that longing reveal about the state of your faith? Make a concrete plan to cultivate that desire today.
?
The Mu'tazila rejected ru'yah because they thought they knew how vision must work. Are there any areas of your belief where you impose your own limited understanding over clear revelation? Are you willing to submit your intellect to Allah's words, even when they contradict your assumptions? Repent from any such intellectual pride and resolve to follow the truth wherever it leads.

The Syllogistic Proof for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
A logical syllogism extracted from the Quranic verse establishing the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic verse implicitly contains a categorical syllogism demonstrating that seeing Allah is possible.

Core Idea
What is conditioned on the possible is itself possible.

The commentator extracts a formal syllogism from Surat al-A'raf. In this lesson, we examine how the verse's conditional structure yields a logical proof for the possibility of ru'ya, which is a key point of debate in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Carefully read the Quranic verse and its literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If the mountain remains, then you will see Me.'
2
Identify the syllogism elements
The passage states that the verse contains a syllogism with an omitted major premise. Identify the minor premise: Allah conditioned ru'ya on the mountain's stability, which is possible.
The major premise is: 'Everything conditioned on the possible is possible.'
3
Understand the definition of condition
The commentator defines condition: the conditioned occurs upon the occurrence of the condition. The impossible cannot occur under any condition, so if ru'ya were impossible, it could not be conditioned.
4
Restore the full syllogism
Write out the complete syllogism with the implied major premise: Minor: Ru'ya is conditioned on X (possible). Major: Whatever is conditioned on the possible is possible. Conclusion: Ru'ya is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات

ورَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنظُرْ إِلَيْكَ قَالَ لَن تَرَينِي وَلَكِنِ انظُرْ إِلَى الْجَبَلِ فَإِنِ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ, فَسَوْفَ تَركني ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٤٣] . وتقرير الدلالة منه: أنَّه إشارة إلى قياس حذفتْ كُبراه للعِلم بها، ترتيبه : الله تعالى عَلَّقَ رؤية ذاته المُقدَّسة على استقرار الجبل حالَ تجلّيه تعالى له، وهو أمر ممكن في نفسه ضرورة، وكلُّ ما عُلِّق على المُمكن لا يكون إلَّا ممكنا لأنَّ معنى التعليق: الإخبار بأنَّ المُعلَّق يقعُ على تقدير وقوعِ المُعلَّقِ عليه، والمحال لا يقع على شيء من التقادير ; فلو لم تكن الرُّؤية ممكنة لزم الخُلْف في خبره تعالى، ــ وهو محال ، ولو كانت ممتنعة فى الدُّنيا لما سألها موسى ، ولا يجوز على أحدٍ من الأنبياء الجهل بشيء من أحكام الألوهية، وخصوصاً بما يجب له تعالى وما يستحيل،
Section on Divinities. And your Lord said, 'Show me, let me look at You.' He said, 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me.' [Quran 7:143]. And the extraction of the indication from it: that it is a reference to a syllogism whose major premise was omitted due to knowledge of it. Its arrangement: Allah the Exalted conditioned the vision of His sacred essence upon the mountain's stability at the time of His manifestation to it, and that is a possible matter in itself necessarily, and everything that is conditioned upon the possible is nothing but possible, because the meaning of condition is informing that the conditioned occurs upon the assumption of the occurrence of the condition, and the impossible does not occur under any assumption. So if the vision were not possible, there would be contradiction in the news of the Exalted, and that is impossible. And if it were impossible in this world, Moses would not have asked for it, and it is not permissible for any of the prophets to be ignorant of any of the rulings of divinity, especially what is obligatory for Him and what is impossible.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) from the Quranic verse for the possibility of seeing Allah (ru'ya). It first presents a categorical syllogism (qiyas): (1) Allah conditioned the vision of Himself on the stability of the mountain, which is possible. (2) Whatever is conditioned on the possible is itself possible. Therefore, the vision is possible. Then it offers two reductio arguments. First: if the vision were not possible, Allah's statement that it would occur upon the mountain's stability would be false, which is impossible. Second: if the vision were absolutely impossible in this world, Moses (peace be upon him) would not have asked for it; but since he is a prophet who knows what befits Allah and what does not, his request indicates that the vision is possible. These arguments work together to refute the Mu'tazili claim that ru'ya is impossible.

Key Terms
قياس
تعليق
ممكن
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe the verse proves seeing Allah is impossible because Moses was denied.
Correction: The verse uses a conditional: if the mountain remains, then you will see Me. This indicates that seeing Allah is possible in principle, but contingent on a condition that was not met in this world.

Checkpoint
What is the implicit major premise in the syllogism from the verse?
A) Everything impossible is not seen.
B) Everything conditioned on the possible is possible.
C) Whatever is seen is possible.
D) Allah's speech is truthful.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse implicitly contains a valid syllogism proving that seeing Allah is possible, contrary to the Mu'tazili position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If seeing Allah is rationally possible, do you truly long for that vision as the greatest reward? Or have you let doubts or heedlessness weaken your desire? What can you do today to make that hope more real in your heart?
?
The logical certainty in this proof is unshakeable. Are you as certain of meeting your Lord as you are of a mathematical truth? If not, what is holding you back from that level of faith?
?
You now know that Allah did not deny vision outright but attached it to a condition. Does this conditional promise stir a deeper love in you for Allah, who veils Himself yet offers hope? How can you use this hope to transform your worship this week?

Two Reductio Arguments for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Two reductio ad absurdum arguments reinforcing the possibility of seeing Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two reductio ad absurdum arguments that reinforce the conclusion that seeing Allah is possible, based on the impossibility of falsehood in Allah's news and prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
If ru'ya were impossible, Allah's word would be false and a prophet would be ignorant.

After the syllogism, the commentator strengthens the proof with two arguments that show the absurd consequences of denying the possibility of ru'ya. These arguments rely on the impossibility of contradiction in divine speech and the perfect knowledge of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the two reductio arguments. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then impossible.'
2
Analyze the first reductio (khluf)
The first argument: if ru'ya were impossible, then Allah's conditional statement in the verse would be false, leading to a contradiction (khluf) in divine news, which is impossible.
Khluf here means a falsehood in Allah's speech, which is impossible.
3
Analyze the second reductio (Musa's request)
The second argument: if ru'ya were absolutely impossible in this world, Moses would not have asked for it. But he did ask, and prophets cannot be ignorant of divine rulings, so ru'ya must be possible.
Prophets are infallible in matters of divinity.
4
Connect both arguments
Both reductios lead to the same conclusion: the impossibility of ru'ya is impossible. They complement the syllogism by addressing different aspects (divine speech and prophetic knowledge).

Source Text

?

?

?
Prophets cannot be ignorant of divine rulings. You are not a prophet, but you are responsible for learning what is obligatory for Allah and what is impossible. What specific belief about Allah's attributes are you ignorant of, and what will you do today to remove that ignorance?

The Quranic Verse and Imam Malik's Argument for Ru'ya
Malik argues from Quranic verses that believers will see Allah on the Day of Judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Malik uses the contrast between believers and disbelievers in the Quran to argue for the beatific vision.

Core Idea
The reproach of veiling disbelievers implies believers see Allah.

In this lesson, we examine one of the strongest scriptural proofs for the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. Imam Malik's reasoning from the Quranic verses on the Day of Judgment establishes that the believer's vision is not only possible but necessary for the logical contrast with the disbeliever's veiling. Let's read the original text.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Malik's statement
Begin by reading the Arabic text of the verse and Imam Malik's commentary. Notice how he connects the two Quranic verses.
The word 'nazira' is taken literally as 'looking' (seeing).
2
Understand Malik's logical reasoning
Malik argues that the reproach of disbelievers being veiled (mahjubun) would be meaningless if believers did not see Allah. This is an inference from contrast.
If both groups were veiled, there would be no distinction.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof establishes the necessity of vision for believers. Ask yourself: does this argument from contrast hold if vision is only metaphorical?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذٍ نَاضِرَةُ إِلَى رَبِّهَا ناظرة ﴾ [القيامة] . قال مالك بن أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه: لما حجب أعداءه فلم يروه، تجلّى لأوليائه حتّى رأوه، ولو لم ير المؤمنونَ ربَّهم يوم القيامة لم يُعيَّر الكفار بالحجاب فقال : كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَيذِ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ ﴾ [المطفين: ١٥] . وقال الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه : لما حجبَ الله قوما بالشخط دلَّ على أنَّ قوما يرونه بالرّضا، ثُمَّ قال : أَما والله لو لم يوقِن محمد بن إدريس بأنَّه يَرى ربَّه في المعاد لما عبده في الدُّنيا.

وقال محمد بن الفضل: كما حَجَبَهُم في الدُّنيا عن نور توحيده، حَجَبَهُم في الآخرة عن رؤيته .

وأمَّا السُّنَّة فكحديث: إنَّكم سترون ربَّكم كما ترون القمر ليلة.
And among them is His statement, the Exalted: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord' [Quran 75:22-23]. Malik ibn Anas, may Allah be pleased with him, said: When He veiled His enemies so they did not see Him, He manifested to His friends until they saw Him. And if the believers did not see their Lord on the Day of Resurrection, the disbelievers would not be reproached with the veil when He said: 'No indeed, they are on that day veiled from their Lord' [Quran 83:15]. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: When Allah veiled some people with anger, it indicates that some people see Him with pleasure. Then he said: By Allah, if Muhammad ibn Idris (himself) were not certain that he would see his Lord in the afterlife, he would not have worshipped Him in this world. And Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: Just as He veiled them in this world from the light of His tawhid, He veiled them in the afterlife from seeing Him. As for the Sunna, there is the hadith: 'Indeed, you will see your Lord as you see the moon on a night...'
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage presents additional scriptural proofs for the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife. It begins with the Quranic verse from Surat al-Qiyama where faces are described as 'looking at their Lord', taking the word 'nazira' in its literal sense of vision. Then it cites three early authorities: (1) Malik ibn Anas argues from the contrast in Surat al-Mutaffifin—if believers did not see Allah, the reproach of disbelievers being veiled would lose its force. (2) Al-Shafi'i reasons that if Allah veils some out of anger, then others must see Him out of pleasure; he also testifies that his own certainty of seeing Allah motivated his worship. (3) Muhammad ibn al-Fadl draws an analogy between veiling in this world (from the light of tawhid) and veiling in the next (from vision). Finally, a prophetic hadith compares the clarity of the vision to seeing the moon. Together, these evidences build a cumulative case for the Ash'ari doctrine of beatific vision.

Key Terms
رؤية
حجاب
نظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core argument attributed to Imam Malik in this passage?
A) The verse from Surat al-Qiyama directly proves ru'ya.
B) The reproach of disbelievers being veiled implies believers see Allah.
C) The consensus of the Salaf proves the vision.
D) The hadith about the moon settles the debate.

Key Takeaway
Imam Malik demonstrates that the Quranic contrast between believers and disbelievers on the Day of Judgment necessitates that believers see Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly knew that on the Day of Judgment you will see your Lord with your own eyes, how would that change the way you seek Him today? Are you living with a longing for that vision, or are you distracted by temporary veils of this world? What specific veil—a sin, a preoccupation, a comfort—must you remove to prepare for that meeting?
?
The disbelievers are veiled from Allah as a punishment. Is there any veil you are currently placing between yourself and Allah through your choices or neglect? What is one concrete step you can take today to tear down that veil and draw closer to Him?
?
Imam Malik’s argument turns on the contrast between believers and disbelievers. What does your daily life—your habits, priorities, and feelings—reveal about which group you truly resemble? If your heart were examined tonight, would it be found longing for the vision of Allah or content with being veiled?

Arguments of al-Shafi'i, Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, and the Prophetic Hadith
Three additional proofs: al-Shafi'i's logical deduction, Muhammad ibn al-Fadl's analogy, and the prophetic hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the supplementary proofs for ru'ya from early scholars and the Prophet's hadith.

Core Idea
The certainty of seeing Allah in the afterlife motivates sincere worship.

Having established the foundational proof from Malik, we now turn to additional scriptural arguments. Al-Shafi'i presents a logical deduction from the concept of divine veiling, while Muhammad ibn al-Fadl draws an analogy with veiling in this world. Finally, the prophetic hadith gives a vivid simile for the clarity of the vision. Let's explore these together.

Guided Path
1
Read the statements of Shafi'i, Ibn al-Fadl, and the hadith
Read the Arabic text carefully. Notice the different styles: logical deduction, analogy, and prophetic simile.
Each proof builds on the same theme of veiling and vision.
2
Understand al-Shafi'i's logical inference
Al-Shafi'i argues that if some are veiled with anger (sakht), then others must see Him with pleasure (rida). He also adds a personal testimony linking certainty of vision to worship.
This is a rational inference from a revealed premise.
3
Reflect on the hadith simile
The hadith compares seeing Allah to seeing the moon. Consider what aspect is being emphasized: clarity, certainty, or ease?
The simile is not about modality but about vividness.

Source Text

ومنها قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَيذٍ نَاضِرَةُ إِلَى رَبِّهَا ناظرة ﴾ [القيامة] . قال مالك بن أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه: لما حجب أعداءه فلم يروه، تجلّى لأوليائه حتّى رأوه، ولو لم ير المؤمنونَ ربَّهم يوم القيامة لم يُعيَّر الكفار بالحجاب فقال : كَلَّا إِنَّهُمْ عَن رَّبِّهِمْ يَوْمَيذِ لَمَحْجُوبُونَ ﴾ [المطفين: ١٥] . وقال الشافعي رضي الله تعالى عنه : لما حجبَ الله قوما بالشخط دلَّ على أنَّ قوما يرونه بالرّضا، ثُمَّ قال : أَما والله لو لم يوقِن محمد بن إدريس بأنَّه يَرى ربَّه في المعاد لما عبده في الدُّنيا.

?

?
The hadith compares seeing Allah to seeing the moon—clear and undeniable. Do you have that clarity about the afterlife? If not, what doubts are clouding your vision, and how will you seek certainty from the Qur'an and Sunnah starting today?

The Occurrence and Possibility of the Prophet's Seeing Allah
Defines thabata and presents the evidence for ru'yah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of thabata in the context of ru'yah and why its occurrence implies rational possibility.

Core Idea
The Prophet's seeing Allah in this world is both proven and rationally possible.

The poem states that the Prophet saw his Lord in this world. But what does 'thabata' mean? And how do we reconcile this with rational possibility? This lesson unpacks the first part of the commentary, explaining the terminology and the preponderant evidence from the hadith of Ibn Abbas.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage from the beginning to the statement of Ma'mar ibn Rashid. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice the concise style—each clause builds on the previous.
2
Identify the definition of thabata
Find where the author explains that thabata means occurrence and takes place. Understand that occurrence implies possibility, not the reverse.
This is a logical relationship: if something happens, it must be possible.
3
Examine the preponderant view and evidence
Note that most scholars hold the Prophet saw Allah with his eyes, citing Ibn Abbas. The author stresses this is taken only from hearing (transmission).
4
Understand the response to Aisha's denial
See why Ibn Abbas is given precedence over Aisha: he is an affirmant (muthbit) and Ma'mar supports his knowledge.
This shows how scholars weigh evidence in such matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيانه أن رؤية النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لربه في الدنيا ليلة الإسراء والمعراج معنى (ثَبَتَتْ) أي: حصلتْ ووقعت لنبينا في الدُّنيا ليلة الإسراء، والوقوع يستلزم الإمكان، بخلاف العكس، والراجح عند أكثر العلماء أنه رأى ربه بعيني رأسه (۱) ؛ لحديث ابن عباس وغيره ، وهذا لا يؤخذ إلا بالسَّماع منه ؛ فلا ينبغي أن يتشكك فيه، ولما نفت عائشة وقوعها له لا قدم ابن عباس عليها ؛ لأنَّه مُثبِتُ ، حَتَّى قال مَعْمَر بن راشد(۲): ما عائشةُ عندنا بأعلم من ابن عباس، وأما حديث «واعلموا أَنَّكُم لَنْ تَروا رَبَّكُم حتّى تموتوا (۳) فإنَّه وإن أفاد أنَّ الرُّؤية في الدُّنيا وإنْ جازت عقلا فقد امتنعت سمعاً ، لكن من أثبتها للنَّبيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم له أن يقول: إِنَّ الْمُتَكَلِّمَ لا يدخل في عموم كلامه . وهذا بيان لزمانها ، والأول مكانها ، والآخرة من النفخة على ما يأتي. وعلى هذا لا تقدير مضاف عند قوله: (للمختار دنيا ثبتت أي: للمختار زمن الدنيا ثبتت ، فالمراد: أنه رآه زمن وجود الدنيا لا في مكانها الأمير والشنواني .
And its explanation is that the seeing of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, of his Lord in this world on the night of al-Isra and al-Mi'raj is the meaning of (thabatat): i.e., it occurred and took place for our Prophet in this world on the night of al-Isra. Occurrence necessitates possibility, contrary to the opposite. And the preponderant opinion according to most scholars is that he saw his Lord with the eyes of his head (1); due to the hadith of Ibn Abbas and others. And this is only taken from hearing from him; so one should not doubt it. And when Aisha denied its occurrence for him, Ibn Abbas gave precedence over her; because he is an affirmant, until Ma'mar ibn Rashid (2) said: 'Aisha is not more knowledgeable with us than Ibn Abbas.' As for the hadith 'Know that you will not see your Lord until you die' (3), although it indicates that seeing in this world, even if rationally possible, is prevented by revelation, but whoever affirms it for the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, may say: 'The speaker does not enter into the generality of his speech.' And this is an explanation of its time, and the first is its place, and the hereafter is after the Trumpet Blast as will come. And according to this, there is no implicit addition in his statement: (for the chosen one in this world it is established), i.e., for the chosen one in the time of this world it is established, so the meaning is: he saw him in the time of the existence of this world, not in its place — as said by al-Amir and al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage defines the meaning of 'thabata' as the occurrence of the ru'yah for the Prophet in this world. It begins by stating that the ru'yah occurred on the night of Isra and Mi'raj, and that occurrence implies rational possibility. Then it presents the preponderant view that the Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes, citing the hadith of Ibn Abbas. The passage then addresses the objection of Aisha, who denied the ru'yah, and explains that Ibn Abbas is given precedence because he is an affirmant of the event, and Ma'mar ibn Rashid supported this. Next, it deals with the hadith 'you will not see your Lord until you die,' which seems to negate ru'yah in this world. The author explains that while the hadith indicates that ru'yah is prevented by revelation (sam'an) despite being rationally possible, the Prophet is excluded from the ruling because the speaker (Allah) does not include himself in the generality of his own speech. This is a known principle in usul. Finally, the passage clarifies that 'in this world' refers to the temporal realm, not the physical places of this world, echoing commentaries by al-Amir and al-Shanwani.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'al-wuqu' yastalzim al-imkan'?
A) Possibility implies occurrence.
B) Occurrence implies possibility.
C) Occurrence and possibility are identical.
D) Neither implies the other.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's seeing Allah in this world is established by evidence and is rationally possible because occurrence implies possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that the Prophet saw Allah on that night, or do you have doubts that you've never confronted? What would it mean for your faith if you completely surrendered to this truth?
?
If you were in Aisha's position, would you have denied the ru'yah based on your own reasoning, or would you submit to the evidence from the Prophet himself? What does that reveal about your attitude toward revelation?
?
The passage says 'this is only taken from hearing from him; so one should not doubt it.' Are there aspects of the Prophet's teachings that you still allow yourself to doubt? How can you train yourself to accept what he transmitted without hesitation?

Reconciling the General Prohibition with the Prophet's Seeing
Reconciles the hadith with the ru'yah using a usul principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith 'you will not see your Lord until you die' is reconciled with the Prophet's ru'yah using the principle that the speaker does not enter into the generality of his speech.

Core Idea
The Prophet's seeing Allah is excluded from the general prohibition because the speaker does not include himself.

The previous lesson established that the Prophet saw Allah in this world. But what about the hadith that says you will not see your Lord until you die? This lesson shows how scholars resolve this apparent contradiction through a principle of usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage addressing the hadith
Read from 'وأما حديث واعلموا...' to the end. Focus on the argument structure.
2
Understand the tension
The hadith seems to deny ru'yah in this world. The author acknowledges that even if rationally possible, it is prevented by revelation (sam'an).
3
Grasp the principle of exception
The author states that whoever affirms ru'yah for the Prophet can say: 'the speaker does not enter into the generality of his speech.' This means Allah, the speaker, does not include Himself in the ruling.
This is a well-known principle: general statements can have exceptions.
4
Note the clarification of time vs place
The author concludes by explaining that 'in this world' refers to the time of this world, not its physical places.
This resolves potential confusion about the Prophet's location during the Mi'raj.

Ruling on Seeing Allah in This World for Others
States that seeing Allah awake is not established for non-prophets, but valid in dreams, with disagreement on awliya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic ruling regarding seeing Allah in this world for non-prophets, including the consensus on its impossibility while awake, the validity in dreams, and the disagreement about the awliya.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah awake is impossible for non-prophets; dreams are valid; awliya are debated.

This passage from Sanusi's Ilahiyyat addresses a sensitive theological question: can anyone besides the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) see Allah in this world? The author clarifies that seeing Allah while awake is not established for anyone else, and those who claim it are misguided. At the same time, he affirms that seeing Allah in a dream is valid and not subject to dispute, because Satan cannot impersonate Allah. Finally, he notes a disagreement among scholars regarding the possibility for the awliya (saints) to see Allah in this world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the section heading 'قسم الإلهيات' which sets the context of divine matters.
The passage is short but dense with rulings.
2
Identify the three scenarios
The passage addresses three distinct situations: seeing Allah awake for non-prophets, seeing Him in a dream, and seeing Him for the awliya. Identify the ruling for each.
The first is denied, the second affirmed, the third debated.
3
Note the consensus and disagreement
Observe that there is a consensus (إطباق المشايخ) on the misguidance of those who claim seeing Allah awake, with some even declaring disbelief. Also note the disagreement among scholars on the awliya.
The phrase 'على قولين للأشعري' indicates two opinions attributed to Imam al-Ash'ari.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it upholds the uniqueness of the Prophet's vision and protects against anthropomorphism, while affirming the possibility of true dreams.
The impossibility of Satan impersonating Allah in dreams is a key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات ولم تثبت في الدنيا لغير نبينا ، على ما في ذلك من الخلاف ، ومَن ادعاها غيره في الدُّنيا يقظةً فهو ضال بإطباق المشايخ ، وذهب الكواشي والمهدوي (۲) إلى تكفيره ، ولا نزاع في وقوعها مناما (۳) وصحتِها ، فإِنَّ الشَّيطان لا يتمثل به تعالى (٤) كالأنبياء عليهم الصَّلاة والسلام، واختلف في وقوعها للأولياء(٥) على قولين للأشعري أرجحهما المنع .
Section of Ilahiyyat. And it is not established in this world for other than our Prophet, despite the disagreement therein. And whoever claims it for other than him in this world while awake is astray by consensus of the shaykhs. Al-Kawashi and al-Mahdawi (2) held his disbelief. And there is no dispute about its occurrence in a dream (3) and its validity, because Satan cannot take the form of Him, exalted (4), unlike the prophets, peace be upon them. And there is disagreement about its occurrence for the awliya (5) on two opinions of al-Ash'ari, the more preponderant of which is prohibition.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage begins with the section heading 'Ilahiyyat' and addresses the ru'yah for others. It first states that seeing Allah in this world while awake is not established for anyone other than the Prophet, and claims to the contrary are contrary to consensus. Some scholars, like al-Kawashi and al-Mahdawi, even declare such claimants disbelievers. Then it clarifies that seeing in a dream is valid and there is no dispute about it because Satan cannot impersonate Allah, unlike the case with prophets. Finally, it mentions the disagreement about whether the awliya can see Allah in this world while awake; al-Ash'ari is reported to have two opinions, and the preponderant one is that it is prohibited.

Correction: The passage confirms that seeing Allah in a dream is valid and there is no dispute about it, because Satan cannot impersonate Allah.

B) He is astray

Key Takeaway
Seeing Allah while awake is impossible for non-prophets, but seeing Him in a dream is valid and undisputed; the case of awliya remains debated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage warns that claiming to have seen Allah awake leads to being astray or even disbelief. Is there any spiritual experience you have exaggerated or claimed for yourself to gain respect? Will you repent and commit to truthfulness in matters of faith?
?
The disagreement among scholars about awliya shows that not all spiritual claims are approved. Are you quick to believe stories of miracles or visions without verifying them against the teachings of reliable scholars? How can you protect your heart from being deceived by false claims?

Rulings on Seeing Allah or the Prophet in Wakefulness
Rulings on claims of seeing Allah or the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rulings on claims of seeing Allah or the Prophet in wakefulness and dreams, and the scholarly disagreement on visions for saints.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah or the Prophet in wakefulness is for our Prophet alone; claims by others are deviance.

This passage tackles a sensitive theological issue: whether anyone besides the Prophet Muhammad can see Allah or the Prophet in wakefulness. The author lays out clear rulings, distinguishing between wakefulness and dreams, and mentions a disagreement among scholars regarding saints. Understanding these rulings helps protect against false claims and maintains respect for prophetic privilege.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Get a sense of the overall rulings before delving into details.
2
Identify the key rulings
Notice the three main parts: (1) wakefulness claims for anyone besides our Prophet are deviant, with some declaring takfir; (2) dreams are valid and undisputed; (3) saintly visions in wakefulness are disputed. Each part has a different weight.
Pay attention to the phrases 'ضال بإطباق المشايخ' and 'ذهب الكَوَّاشي والمَهْدَويُّ إلى تكفيره'.
3
Understand the rationale for dreams
The author explains why dreams are valid: Satan cannot impersonate Allah or the Prophets in dreams. This is a key doctrinal point that distinguishes dreams from wakefulness claims.
Contrast this with the strict ruling on wakefulness.
4
Reflect on the Ash'ari disagreement
The passage ends with al-Ash'ari's two opinions on whether saints can see Allah or the Prophet in wakefulness. Note that the stronger opinion prohibits it. Think about why this matters for theological balance.
This shows that even within a school, there can be internal debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم تثبت في الدنيا لغير نبينا ، على ما في ذلك من الخلاف ، ومَن ادعاها غيره في الدُّنيا يقظةً فهو ضال بإطباق المشايخ ، وذهب الكَوَّاشي والمَهْدَويُّ إلى تكفيره ، ولا نزاع في وقوعها مناما وصحتِها ، فإِنَّ الشَّيطان لا يتمثل به تعالى كالأنبياء عليهم الصَّلاة والسلام، واختلف في وقوعها للأولياء على قولين للأشعري أرجحهما المنع .
And it has not been established in this world for other than our Prophet, despite the disagreement in that, and whoever claims it from others in wakefulness is deviant by the consensus of the shaykhs, and al-Kawashi and al-Mahdawi went to the extent of declaring him an unbeliever, and there is no dispute regarding its occurrence in a dream and its validity, for indeed Satan cannot impersonate Him (Exalted is He) nor the Prophets, peace be upon them, and there is disagreement regarding its occurrence for the saints as two opinions from al-Ash'ari, the more preponderant of which is prohibition.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on claims of seeing Allah or the Prophet in wakefulness and dreams. It begins by stating that such visions in wakefulness have not been established for anyone besides our Prophet Muhammad (saw), regardless of any disagreement on the matter. Whoever claims them for others in wakefulness is considered deviant (ḍāl) by all scholars (mašāyikh). Even stronger, al-Kawashi and al-Mahdawi considered such a person to be an unbeliever (kāfir). Then the author clarifies that there is no dispute regarding the occurrence and validity of seeing the Prophet or Allah in dreams. The reason given is that Satan cannot impersonate Allah or the Prophets in dreams. Finally, the author mentions that al-Ash'ari reported two opinions on whether such visions can occur for saints (awliyā') in wakefulness, with the more preponderant (arjaḥ) opinion being prohibition (man‘). This passage thus establishes strict limits on the possibility of visual contact with the divine or prophetic in wakefulness, while affirming the reality and validity of dreams, and noting the disagreement on saintly vision.

Key Terms
يقظة
منام
ضال
تكفير
أولياء
أشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing the Prophet in a dream is equivalent to seeing him in wakefulness in terms of ruling.
Correction: The passage clearly distinguishes: wakefulness claims for others are deviance/takfir, while dreams are valid and undisputed. They are not equivalent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who claims to have seen the Prophet or Allah in wakefulness?
A) They are considered a pious saint.
B) They are deviant (ḍāl) by consensus, and some scholars declare them an unbeliever.
C) It is permissible if they are truthful.
D) It is a sign of prophecy.

Key Takeaway
Claims of seeing Allah or the Prophet in wakefulness are not accepted for anyone other than the Prophet; such claimants are deviant or even disbelievers, while dreams are valid and saintly visions are disputed with the stronger view being prohibition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly wished for a supernatural spiritual experience to confirm your faith? How does this teaching expose the danger of seeking such signs and the sufficiency of the prophetic message?
?
If you were to see the Prophet in a dream tonight, would it transform your daily obedience to his Sunnah, or would you treat it as a passing curiosity? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart?
?
In a world filled with false claims of divine encounters, how do you guard yourself from being deceived by spiritual pride? This lesson declares wakeful visions for others as deviance—does your soul humbly accept this boundary, or does it chafe against it?

Attribution of Death: Allah as Creator, Angel as Agent
Explains how death is ascribed to Allah as Creator and to the angel as agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why death is attributed both to Allah and to the Angel of Death, and how these attributions are not contradictory.

Core Idea
Death is ultimately from Allah; the Angel is the immediate agent.

This lesson resolves an apparent contradiction between Quranic verses that attribute death to Allah and those that attribute it to the Angel of Death. The text explains that both are correct: Allah is the real Creator, while the Angel is the appointed executor. This distinction preserves divine transcendence and clarifies the role of secondary causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the three distinct attributions mentioned.
2
Identify the three attributions
Find where death is attributed to Allah (verse 39:42), to the Angel of Death (verse 32:11), and to the angel's helpers (verse 6:61).
Each has a different basis: Creator, direct agent, indirect agent.
3
Understand the principle of agency
The passage shows that one act (taking the soul) can be ascribed to multiple agents depending on the level of causation: Allah as the ultimate Creator, the Angel as the direct executor, and helpers as secondary agents.
This resolves any apparent contradiction.
4
Apply to other divine acts
Consider how similar attributions work for other actions like providing sustenance or sending rain. The principle is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا إِسنادُ التّوفّي إليه تعالى في قوله: ﴿اللَّهُ يَتَوَفَّى الْأَنفُسَ حِينَ مَوْتِهَا﴾ [الزمر: ٤٢]؛ فلأنه الخالق الحقيقي المُوجد له، ولما باشره مَلكُ الموتِ أُسند إليه، كقوله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ يَتَوَفَّنَكُم مَّلَكُ الْمَوْتِ الَّذِي وُكِلَ بِكُم﴾ [السجدة: ١١]، كنسبته إلى أعوانه لمعالجتهم نَزْعها في قوله تعالى: ﴿تَوَفَّتْهُ رُسُلُنَا﴾ [الأنعام: ٦١].
As for the attribution of causing death to Him, the Exalted, in His statement: 'Allah takes the souls at the time of their death' [Az-Zumar 39:42], it is because He is the real Creator, the originator of it. And when the Angel of Death directly carries it out, it is attributed to him, as in His statement: 'Say, the Angel of Death who is entrusted with you will take you in full' [As-Sajda 32:11], just as it is attributed to his helpers for their handling of its extraction in His statement: 'Our messengers take him in full' [Al-An'am 6:61].
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is a theological explanation of how the act of causing death is attributed both to Allah and to the Angel of Death. The verse 'Allah takes the souls' attributes death to Allah because He is the true Creator and originator of the act. But when the Angel of Death directly performs the taking, death is attributed to him as the agent, as in the verse 'Say, the Angel of Death will take you in full'. Similarly, death is attributed to the angel's helpers when they handle the extraction of the soul, as in 'Our messengers take him in full'. Thus, the attribution is based on agency while Allah is the ultimate cause. This resolves apparent contradictions between verses.

Key Terms
التوفي
ملك الموت
الخالق الحقيقي
أعوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think attributing death to the Angel of Death contradicts attributing it to Allah.
Correction: The text clarifies that both attributions are valid: Allah as the Creator and ultimate cause, the Angel as the direct agent.

Checkpoint
Why is death attributed to Allah in the verse 'Allah takes the souls'?
A) Because Allah is the real Creator
B) Because the Angel of Death is not involved
C) Because Allah holds the angel responsible
D) Because death is a punishment

Key Takeaway
The same act of taking a soul is attributed to Allah as the Creator and to the Angel as the agent, reflecting different levels of causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face the death of a loved one, do you truly believe that Allah is the Creator of that death, even while the angel carried it out? How does this shape your grief and trust in His decree? What practical step can you take today to deepen that trust?
?
Reflect on your own mortality. The Angel of Death is entrusted with you. Are you living in a way that you are ready for him to take your soul? What one change in your daily life would make you more prepared?
?
If you attribute events in your life only to secondary causes (people, circumstances), how might you be forgetting Allah's ultimate role as Creator? How can you shift your perspective starting tomorrow to see His hand behind every outcome?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
Proof of Martyrs' Complete Life from the Quran
Sanusi proves martyrs' complete life via Quran and explains its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic proof for the complete life of martyrs and how it involves both body and soul, with full perception.

Core Idea
Martyrs are truly alive with full perception in body and soul.

A foundational belief in Sunni Islam is that martyrs are not dead but alive. This lesson examines the Quranic verse that establishes this truth and explores what kind of life it is—whether merely spiritual or involving both body and soul. Sanusi clarifies the nature of this life and compares it to the even higher state of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: the verse, then its interpretation, then the conclusion about the nature of life.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لظاهر الآية' which grounds the interpretation in the apparent meaning.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
Locate the verse cited (Quran 3:169) and understand how Sanusi uses it as proof. The verse literally says martyrs are alive and provided for.
3
Analyze the nature of martyrs' life
The passage describes the life as real (حقيقة), complete (كاملة), involving perception (knowledge, hearing, sight), and encompassing both spirit and body. Contrast this with an incomplete life that is only spiritual.
This refutes the idea that martyrs' life is merely metaphorical.
4
Consider the comparison with prophets
The final sentence states that prophets have an even more complete life because they share the martyrs' station and exceed it. Reflect on why this matters for theology.
This affirms the superiority of prophethood over martyrdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم السمعيات ( بِالحَيَاةِ) الكاملة (۱) ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تَحْسَبَنَّ الَّذِينَ قُتِلُوا فِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ أَمْوَاتَا بَلْ أَحْيَاءٌ عِندَ رَبِّهِمْ يُرْزَقُونَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٦٩] . وحياتهم حقيقة ، لظاهر الآية ، وأنَّهم يُرزَقون ممَّا يَشْتَهُونَ كما تُرزَقُ الأحياء بالأكل والشرب واللباس وغيرها . وحياتهم كاملة التَّامة الإدراكات ، كالعلم والسماع والإبصار، وهي بالروح والجسد معا لا الناقصة المتعلقة بالروح فقط . والأنبياء أكمل حياة من الشهداء، لأنهم حازوا مقام الشُّهداء وزادوا عليهم.
Section of Audible Matters. With complete life; due to His saying the Exalted: 'And do not think that those who were killed in the way of Allah are dead; rather they are alive with their Lord receiving provision.' [Al 'Imran 169]. And their life is real, due to the apparent meaning of the verse, and that they are provided with what they desire just as the living are provided with eating, drinking, clothing, and other things. And their life is complete, perfect in perceptions, such as knowledge, hearing, and sight, and it is by the spirit and the body together, not the incomplete life attached only to the spirit. And the prophets are more complete in life than the martyrs, because they obtained the station of the martyrs and exceeded them.
Source pages p. 567

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation of the life of martyrs. It opens by affirming that martyrs possess complete life, citing Quran 3:169 as evidence. The commentator then explains that their life is true and real because the verse is taken at face value. He elaborates that martyrs are provided with what they desire, just as living humans eat, drink, and wear clothes. Next, he defines the completeness of this life: it involves full perceptions like knowledge, hearing, and sight, and it is a life connected to both spirit and body, not merely to the spirit alone. Finally, he notes that prophets have an even more complete life than martyrs, since they share the martyrs' station and surpass it.

Key Terms
حياة الشهداء
حقيقة
كاملة
الروح والجسد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the martyrs' life is merely spiritual or metaphorical.
Correction: The passage stresses it is real and involves both body and soul, as per the apparent meaning of the verse.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for affirming that the martyrs' life is real and not metaphorical?
A) Consensus of scholars
B) The apparent meaning of the Quranic verse
C) Rational proof
D) The sayings of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Martyrs enjoy a real, complete life with both body and soul, as proven by the Quran, and prophets have an even more complete life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The martyrs gave up everything for Allah and now enjoy a complete life with full perception. What are you holding onto that prevents you from sacrificing your comfort, time, or reputation for His sake? Write down one specific attachment you need to loosen.
?
If you truly believed that the martyrs are alive and aware of their provision, would you envy their state? Or are you too attached to this world's pleasures to desire what they have? Ask your heart honestly.
?
The prophets have an even more complete life than the martyrs. Do you long to be among their company, or have you settled for a lazy faith that expects nothing more? What step will you take today to draw closer to their rank?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
The External Fixity of Realities
Sanusi asserts the mind-independent fixity of the realities of existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the realities of existent things are fixed externally, independent of mental consideration.

Core Idea
Realities are not mental fictions—they exist outside the mind.

This lesson tackles a foundational principle of Sunni metaphysics: the objective existence of the essences of things. The author asserts that every existent's reality is fixed externally, regardless of our thoughts or assumptions. This affirms a realist epistemology against subjectivist or nominalist tendencies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's direct statement.
Notice the grammatical parsing: the predicate 'fixed in external existence' completes the subject 'the existent'.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that the reality of every existent is fixed in external existence and in the matter itself (nafs al-amr). This is the central thesis.
3
Notice the negation
The fixity does not depend on anyone's consideration or assumption. This rules out subjective relativism about essences.
The Arabic uses 'min ghayr naẓar' (without regard).
4
Reflect on the examples
The author gives examples: man, horse, sky, earth. These are not just mental constructs but real entities in themselves.
Think about how this connects to our everyday knowledge of objects.
5
Consider the doctrinal significance
This principle underpins the validity of knowledge and language: when we say 'man' or 'horse', we refer to a fixed reality, not a mere mental concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان أن حقيقة كل موجود ثابتة في الخارج والعيان. (وثابت في الخارج) خبر قوله: (الموجود) الواقع مبتدأ، يعني: أنا نقطع ونتحقق أن حقيقة كل موجود ثابتة ومتحققة في الخارج ونفس الأمر، واجبة كانت أو ممكنة، من غير نظر إلى اعتبار المعتبر ولا فرض الفارض، فما نعتقده حقائق الأشياء ونسميه بالأسماء من الإنسان والفرس والسماء والأرض... أمور موجودة في نفس الأمر.
Statement that the reality of every existent is fixed in external existence and in the eye. (And fixed in external existence) is the predicate of his saying: (the existent) which is the subject, meaning: we are certain and verify that the reality of every existent is fixed and realized in external existence and in the matter itself, whether it is necessary or possible, without regard to the consideration of the considerer or the assumption of the assumer. So what we believe as realities of things and name with names, such as man, horse, sky, and earth... are existent things in the matter itself.
Source pages p. 580

Commentary
This passage presents an affirmation about the metaphysical status of existent things. It begins with a heading: 'Statement that the reality of every existent is fixed in external existence and in the eye.' Then it provides a grammatical parsing: the phrase 'Wa thâbitun fî al-khârij' (and fixed in external existence) is the predicate of the subject 'al-mawjûd' (the existent) found in the earlier matn. The commentary explains the meaning: we are certain and verify that the reality (haqîqa) of every existent—whether necessary (i.e., God) or possible (i.e., creatures)—is fixed and realized in external existence and in the matter itself (nafs al-amr). This fixity does not depend on someone's mental consideration (i'tibâr al-mu'tabir) or on someone's assumption (fard al-fârid). Therefore, the things we believe as realities and name with specific names, such as 'man,' 'horse,' 'sky,' 'earth,' are existent things in the matter itself, not merely mental constructs. The passage emphasizes the objective, mind-independent existence of the essences of things, consistent with Sunni realism.

Key Terms
ثابت
خارج
حقيقة
موجود
نفس الأمر
اعتبار
فرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the reality of things is merely a mental concept or depends on our thoughts.
Correction: The passage affirms that the reality of every existent is fixed externally, regardless of mental consideration or assumption.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the reality of existent things?
A) They are fixed only in the mind.
B) They are fixed in external existence independently of mental consideration.
C) They are fixed only for God but not for creatures.
D) They are fixed only by the assumption of an assumer.

Key Takeaway
The realities of things are fixed in external existence, independent of any mental consideration or assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What realities do you know to be objectively true—like the existence of your Creator or your own mortality—but sometimes live as if they were optional beliefs? Where in your daily life do you treat fixed realities as if they depend on your mood or convenience?
?
If the reality of everything is fixed ‘without regard to the consideration of the considerer or the assumption of the assumer,’ then what about your own judgments? When have you mistaken a mere assumption for a fixed truth, and how did that affect your faith or conduct?
?
Your names and labels for things—like ‘man’ or ‘sky’—correspond to real essences in the external world. But does your reverence for God’s names reflect His absolute reality, or have you reduced His names to empty terms? What would it mean for you to truly treat His reality as fixed and unshakeable?
